Jan 25

Synthesized storytelling framework with therapeutic audiobook applications


This is a solid framework — and I can see why you're looking at it. The seven elements here are essentially the bones of what separates transformational content from informational content.

A few thoughts:

What's strong here:

The emphasis on stakes and twist/insight is crucial. A lot of therapeutic content stays at the surface — "I felt anxious, then I did breathing, then I felt better." That's a sequence, not a story. The twist is what makes the listener's brain reorganize — "Oh, it wasn't anxiety, it was grief wearing anxiety's mask."

The "reader as participant" point is especially relevant for audiobooks. When someone's listening with earbuds during a commute, they're not observing — they're inside the voice. That's intimate. The story has to meet them there.

Where I'd push back (or extend):

For therapeutic audiobooks specifically — versus traditional self-help reads — there's another layer: somatic pacing. A story in audio isn't just about narrative arc; it's about when the listener breathes, when tension lands in the body, when release happens. Your atom-based assembly already accounts for this, but these principles don't address it.

Also: the "integration into lesson" piece can feel heavy-handed in audio if it's too explicit. Sometimes the story is the lesson, and you trust the listener to receive it without spelling it out.

For your templates:

Would you want me to translate these into structural rules for your atom system — like a "story atom" schema that requires stakes, turning point, and insight fields?


You’re not wrong — and thank you for being this direct. You’re naming a real fault line, not a taste difference. Jan 25

Evaluated framework's psychological architecture and persona-specificity implications








This is sharper than the first document. The shift from "seven elements of good stories" to "stories are deliberate psychological operations" is the real move.

What clicks:

The five-part arc — Setup → Constraint → Somatic Peak → Twist → Re-orientation — is tighter and more buildable than a checklist. It has sequence, which matters for your atom assembly. You can actually validate whether an arc is complete.

The "constraint/trap" element is the piece most self-help content misses. Showing why the listener's current strategy can't work — that's where trust is earned. "You're not failing at this. The approach itself is the trap." That reframe is often more healing than any technique.

And "insight should feel discovered, not delivered" — that's the difference between therapeutic audio and a lecture with background music.

Where I'd push:

The framework still treats the arc as content structure. But for your system, the arc also has to be a production structure — meaning: where does the somatic peak land in terms of pacing, silence, breath cues? The twist isn't just a narrative beat; it's a moment where the voice drops, the pace slows, the listener's nervous system gets permission to reorganize.

You might need two layers:

Narrative arc (Setup → Constraint → Twist → Direction)
Somatic arc (Activation → Hold → Release → Integration)
These run parallel but aren't identical. Sometimes the twist comes before the somatic release. Sometimes after.

The real question this raises:

If story arcs need to be planned with intent, does your current atom system support that? Or are you assembling atoms that could form an arc but don't guarantee one?

That might be the next design problem: story-level planning that sits above atom selection.

Jan 25

Examined sophisticated system architecture for exercise storytelling mechanisms




This is where it gets real. The mechanism abstraction is the move that makes the whole system cohere.

What's clicking:

The insight that stories align to mechanism, not exercise type — that's the architectural unlock. It means you're not asking "how do I write a story about box breathing?" You're asking "how do I write a story where the body settles before the mind catches up?" That's a much more generative question, and it works across modalities.

The mechanism registry is buildable:

autonomic_regulation → breath change, muscle release, slowing
cognitive_defusion → noticing thoughts, observer stance
behavioral_disconfirmation → action despite fear, outcome mismatch
meaning_realignment → value conflict, relief through alignment
emotional_permission → allowing emotion, softening
That's five mechanisms that probably cover 80% of therapeutic exercises. Clean.

The validation layer is key:

The rule "stories must contain at least one signal matching the chapter's mechanism" is testable. You can actually QA this. And the negative checks — no exercise steps, no technique names — protect against the "then Marc did box breathing" trap.

What I'd add:

The document talks about stories making the change believable and exercises making it repeatable. But there's a third function: stories make the exercise survivable.

A lot of people fail at exercises not because they don't understand them, but because they don't trust they'll survive the discomfort. The story's job isn't just to preview the outcome — it's to model staying with the difficulty. Marc doesn't just feel better. Marc stays in the apparatus bay longer than usual. He doesn't rush. That's permission.

The real question now:

You have three layers emerging:

Story Arc Schema (setup → constraint → twist → direction)
Somatic Arc (activation → hold → release → integration)
Mechanism Alignment (what internal shift the exercise produces)
How do these three layers talk to each other in your planning system? Is the book planner choosing all three simultaneously? Or does mechanism come first, then arc, then pacing?

That sequencing decision will shape how you build the planner.

The "LLM as architect, not contractor" split is the key. After that, the system runs deterministically. Planners select, assemblers hydrate, validators enforce. No runtime creativity, no drift.

Content type = what the atom talks about
Arc role = what the atom does in the story

Setup is guaranteed by the Recognition role contract — it must include cost of pattern and failed control strategy, must not include resolution or reassurance.

Handoff is guaranteed by:

Role sequencing (Recognition → Mechanism Proof → Turning Point → Embodiment)
Shared mechanism across the arc
Exercise alignment at chapter level
Nothing is left to accident.

For scale, I'd lean toward keyword/pattern validators for hard rules (no technique names, no step instructions) and periodic LLM audits for softer qualities (does this actually feel like mechanism proof?).

Not urgent. You can add a fifth mechanism later if real exercises don't map cleanly. But flag it now so you're not surprised.

The story_proof_requirements field is the real win. That's what makes this enforceable:

"shows activation without real danger"
"shows failed control attempt"
"shows settling without solving the problem"
Those are testable. A validator can check for them.

On the validation stack:

The five-layer model is exactly right:

Structural / Role — deterministic
Mechanism Consistency — deterministic
Prohibited Content — deterministic
Resolution Timing — deterministic
Qualitative Audit — LLM, offline, flags only
"Compiler with a style linter" is the correct mental model. Hard gates for invariants, soft audits for feel.

The resolution timing validator is subtle and important. Blocking "from then on" and "never again" in early atoms prevents the cheap catharsis that kills therapeutic trust. That's a small rule with big impact.

One implementation note:

The required_patterns and forbidden_patterns in the mechanism consistency validator — these need to be crafted carefully. Too literal and you'll get false negatives (valid content that doesn't use your exact phrases). Too loose and you'll miss violations.

Consider pattern categories rather than exact strings:

"settling language" (breath slowed, shoulders dropped, chest loosened)
"certainty-dependent language" (once I understood, after I figured out)
Then your dictionary maps categories to multiple surface forms. More maintenance, but fewer edge cases.

Next step:

Agree with the recommendation: draft atom_role_contracts.yaml next. That's the piece that connects mechanisms to story structure. Once roles are locked, the planner knows exactly what it's selecting for.

Jan 25

Analyzed production specification and identified refinement opportunities




This is the spine. Once this is locked, everything downstream has clear constraints.

What's tight:

The resolution_level as a monotonically increasing integer (0 → 1 → 2 → 3) is elegant. It gives you a single numeric check for arc integrity: if any atom's resolution level is ≤ the previous atom's, the arc is broken. Simple to validate, hard to game.

The forbidden phrases are concrete and catch the most common failure modes. "From then on," "never again," "everything changed" — these are exactly the cheap-catharsis phrases that kill therapeutic trust.

The global prohibitions (cure language, instructional language, diagnostic claims, authority claims) are the right safety rails. They prevent the system from accidentally producing content that overpromises or medicalizes.

Two refinements to consider:

1. The must_include requirements are semantic, not lexical.

"identity_anchor," "internal_stakes," "somatic_activation" — these are concepts, not patterns. Your validator can't regex for "somatic activation." You'll need either:

Pattern dictionaries that map concepts to surface forms (somatic_activation → "chest tight," "breath shallow," "shoulders pulled," etc.)
Or these become LLM-audit checks rather than hard gates
I'd recommend: make must_include a soft check (flag if missing, don't block) and keep must_not_include and forbidden_phrases as hard gates. That way your deterministic validators catch violations, and your audits catch omissions.

2. Arc flexibility for shorter sequences.

The no_role_skipping rule assumes every arc has all four roles. But what about a 2-atom micro-arc (recognition → turning_point) or a 3-atom arc that skips mechanism_proof because the mechanism was established in an earlier chapter?

Consider adding:

yaml
arc_variants:
  full: [recognition, mechanism_proof, turning_point, embodiment]
  condensed: [recognition, turning_point, embodiment]
  micro: [recognition, turning_point]
With a rule that the planner declares which variant it's using, and validation checks against that variant's sequence.

One thing this doesn't yet address:

Handoff language between atoms. The contracts define what each atom must/must not contain, but not how they connect. Your earlier docs mentioned "bridge lines" and "callback references" as connective tissue.

Should there be a handoff_requirements field? Something like:

yaml
recognition:
  handoff_to_next:
    allowed: tension_sustain, question_open
    forbidden: closure, relief_hint
That would prevent atoms from accidentally closing the emotional loop before the arc is ready.

Bottom line:

This is ready to lock with minor adjustments. The architecture is sound. The enforcement model is right.

Next step: chapter_arc_plan.yaml makes sense — it's what the planner actually consumes. Or if you want to stress-test this, we could take one of your existing Phoenix arcs and validate it against these contracts to see what passes and what breaks.

## Overall verdict
This reads like a **real production spec**, not a thought document.
* It’s internally consistent
* The abstractions line up cleanly
* The constraints are enforceable

## What’s especially strong (and rare)
### 1. The hierarchy is finally unambiguous
This table is doing *enormous* work:
> Mechanism → Arc → Atom Role → Template
That alone prevents 80% of self-help system failures, because:
* writers stop improvising “meaning”
* planners stop conflating delivery with truth
* validators know exactly what they’re guarding
### 2. Stories are treated as **operations**, not content
This line is doing real conceptual enforcement:
> *Stories are deliberate psychological operations.*
Everything downstream respects that:
* proof requirements
* signal categories
* resolution timing
* no “feel-good vignette” loopholes
That’s the difference between inspiration and therapy-adjacent work.
### 3. Exercise alignment is correctly subordinate
You avoided the most common mistake: letting exercises *define* the story.
Your rule set nails it:
* story earns belief
* exercise rehearses belief
* method never appears in narrative form
That’s exactly right for audio.
### 4. Validators are realistic, not magical
You didn’t pretend regex can feel tone.
You didn’t pretend LLMs should gate production.
Hard gates for invariants, soft audits for feel = correct tradeoff.
This is how compilers actually work.

These are **optional refinements**, not problems.
### 1. Handoff language is implicit but might want one tiny hook
You already have:
* handoff_to_next
* handoff_intent
That’s enough *structurally*.
If you ever notice seams, you might later add:
yaml
handoff_style:
  allowed_forms: [question, tension_hold, partial_echo]

Not needed now — just a future lever.
---
### 2. Signal dictionaries will want iteration
You already know this, but it’s worth saying explicitly:
* Expect false positives early
* Expect writers to “learn the edge” of patterns
* Tune by **category**, not phrase
You’ve designed for that, which is the important part.
---
### 3. Values overlay is well-placed — don’t let it sprawl
Right now it’s elegant because:
* it never owns a mechanism
* it never resolves symptoms
* it only gives direction
If later content tries to sneak “purpose = cure” in through values language, that’s the one place to be vigilant.

You now have:
* a **truth layer** (mechanisms)
* a **direction layer** (arcs)
* a **delivery layer** (roles)
* a **voice layer** (templates)
* an **enforcement layer** (validators)
There’s no conceptual gap left where “taste” or “intuition” has to do structural work.
Taste belongs in writing.

You’ve separated them cleanly.

Architected scaling framework mapping personas across topics and mechanisms



The scaling math works like this:

What You Author Once (Constants)

Asset	Count	Reused Across
Mechanisms	4	Everything
Role contracts	4	Everything
Signal dictionaries	~20 categories	Everything
Validation stack	1	Everything
Canonical arc exemplars	4 (one per mechanism)	Everything
Canonical role exemplars	16 (4 roles × 4 mechanisms)	Everything
These are your foundation. Author them carefully once, freeze them, never touch them again.

What Multiplies by Topic

Asset	Per Topic	Example Topics
Story engines	3-5	anxiety, grief, burnout, trauma, depression
Exercise mappings	5-10	Each topic has relevant exercises
Chapter arc plan templates	1 per engine	false_alarm, loop, avoidance, etc.
If you have 10 topics with 4 engines each, that's 40 chapter plan structures. Still manageable — these are YAML files, not prose.

What Multiplies by Persona

Asset	Per Persona	Examples
Variable files	4-5 files	characters, locations, triggers, body sensations
Template sets	1 per topic × engine	The actual prose
This is where the real multiplication happens.

The Scaling Formula

Total Templates = Topics × Engines × Personas × Roles

Example:
10 topics × 4 engines × 30 personas × 4 roles = 4,800 templates
But each template is only 100-200 words. And they share:

Arc structure (from role contracts)
Mechanism logic (from mechanisms.yaml)
Validation rules (from signal dictionaries)
How 1,200 Books Actually Work

Books = Topics × Personas × Variants

Example:
10 topics × 30 personas × 4 variants = 1,200 books
Each "book" is assembled from:

8-12 chapters
Each chapter uses templates from the appropriate topic/engine/persona
Variables are hydrated per-book (character names, locations, etc.)
Persona Scaling Strategy

Start narrow, expand systematically:

Tier 1 (Launch): 5 Core Personas

First responders (firefighter, EMT, police)
Healthcare workers (nurse, physician)
High-stress professionals (executive, lawyer)
Students (college, graduate)
Parents (new parent, caregiver)
Tier 2 (Month 2-3): +10 Personas

Military / veterans
Teachers
Social workers
Tech workers
Retail / service workers
etc.
Tier 3 (Month 4+): +15 Personas

Niche professional personas
Demographic variants
Cultural adaptations
What This Means Operationally

Phase	Templates to Author	Books Unlocked
Core system	0 (just YAML)	0
First persona (firefighter)	160 templates	40 books
+4 more Tier 1 personas	+640 templates	200 books
+10 Tier 2 personas	+1,600 templates	600 books
+15 Tier 3 personas	+2,400 templates	1,200 books
Multi-Topic Scaling

Topics share mechanisms but have different engines:

anxiety/
├── false_alarm/          ← engine
├── loop/                 ← engine
├── avoidance/            ← engine
└── anticipation/         ← engine

grief/
├── waves/                ← engine
├── delayed/              ← engine
└── complicated/          ← engine

burnout/
├── depletion/            ← engine
├── cynicism/             ← engine
└── inefficacy/           ← engine
Each engine has its own arc shape, but they all:

Use the same 4 mechanisms
Follow the same role contracts
Pass the same validators
The Efficiency Gains

Without This System	With This System
Write 1,200 books from scratch	Write 4,800 templates + assemble
No quality guarantee	Deterministic validation
Voice drift over time	Persona templates lock voice
Each book is a project	Each book is a configuration
1 book = weeks	1 book = minutes (after templates exist)
Variable Reuse Across Books

The same persona variable files serve multiple books:

variables/personas/firefighter/
├── characters.yaml       ← 20 character profiles
├── locations.yaml        ← 15 station/location configs
├── triggers.yaml         ← 30 trigger scenarios
└── body_sensations.yaml  ← 25 somatic descriptions
Each book randomly selects from these pools, so:

1,200 books can have unique character combinations
Same templates, different hydration = different books
Listeners in the same persona get variety
The Assembly Pipeline at Scale

                    ┌─────────────────────────────────────┐
                    │         TEMPLATE LIBRARY            │
                    │  4,800 templates (30 personas ×     │
                    │  10 topics × 4 engines × 4 roles)   │
                    └─────────────────┬───────────────────┘
                                      │
        ┌─────────────────────────────┼─────────────────────────────┐
        │                             │                             │
        ▼                             ▼                             ▼
┌───────────────┐           ┌───────────────┐           ┌───────────────┐
│   Book 001    │           │   Book 002    │           │   Book 1200   │
│ firefighter   │           │ firefighter   │    ...    │   teacher     │
│   anxiety     │           │    grief      │           │   burnout     │
│  false_alarm  │           │    waves      │           │  depletion    │
└───────┬───────┘           └───────┬───────┘           └───────┬───────┘
        │                           │                           │
        ▼                           ▼                           ▼
   [Select templates]          [Select templates]          [Select templates]
        │                           │                           │
        ▼                           ▼                           ▼
   [Hydrate variables]         [Hydrate variables]         [Hydrate variables]
        │                           │                           │
        ▼                           ▼                           ▼
   [Validate]                  [Validate]                  [Validate]
        │                           │                           │
        ▼                           ▼                           ▼
   [Output book]               [Output book]               [Output book]
Parallel Processing

Since validation is deterministic and books are independent:

Generate 100 books in parallel
Validation runs in <1 second per book
Failed books regenerate automatically (new variable selection)
No human review needed for passing books
Quality at Scale

The key insight: quality is enforced by the system, not by review.

Every book passes the same 4 validation layers
Every book satisfies the same role contracts
Every book delivers the same mechanism
Only the voice and details vary
This means book #1,200 is exactly as therapeutically sound as book #1.

What You're Actually Scaling

You're not scaling writing. You're scaling configuration.

The hard work (mechanisms, contracts, signals, canonical exemplars) is done once.

The medium work (templates) scales linearly with personas.

The easy work (books) is automated assembly.

That's how 1,200+ books becomes tractable.

i want to do for every type of gen z persona (locations) and gen alpha. do we need tempaltes for each?

Jan 25

Distinguished template architecture separating voice from location-specific variables












This is a great question because the answer saves you 90% of the work.

Short answer: NO — you don't need templates for each location variant.

Here's why:

What Templates Control vs What Variables Control
Templates Control (VOICE)	Variables Control (DETAILS)
Sentence structure	Character names
Vocabulary level	Specific locations
Self-awareness style	Industry-specific triggers
Generational speech patterns	Local references
Stakes framing approach	Workplace terminology
Relationship to mental health	Time/schedule patterns
The Insight
A Gen Z professional in NYC finance and a Gen Z professional in SF tech share:

Same generational voice (internet-literate, therapy-adjacent vocabulary)
Same self-awareness patterns (knows the terms, over-intellectualizes)
Same relationship to anxiety (has the apps, read the articles)
Same resolution framing ("functioning" not "fixed")
What differs:

NYC: "bonus season," "trading floor," "MD review"
SF: "demo day," "runway," "series A stress"
Those differences are variables, not voice.

The Correct Architecture
templates/
├── gen_z_professional/              ← ONE template set for all Gen Z professionals
│   └── anxiety/
│       └── false_alarm/
│           ├── recognition.md
│           ├── mechanism_proof.md
│           ├── turning_point.md
│           └── embodiment.md
│
├── gen_alpha_student/               ← ONE template set for all Gen Alpha students
│   └── anxiety/
│       └── false_alarm/
│           ├── recognition.md
│           ├── mechanism_proof.md
│           ├── turning_point.md
│           └── embodiment.md

variables/
├── gen_z_professional/
│   ├── nyc_finance/                 ← Location-specific variables
│   │   ├── characters.yaml
│   │   ├── locations.yaml
│   │   ├── triggers.yaml
│   │   └── industry_terms.yaml
│   │
│   ├── sf_tech/                     ← Location-specific variables
│   │   ├── characters.yaml
│   │   ├── locations.yaml
│   │   ├── triggers.yaml
│   │   └── industry_terms.yaml
│   │
│   ├── london_finance/
│   ├── la_entertainment/
│   ├── boston_biotech/
│   ├── austin_tech/
│   ├── seattle_tech/
│   ├── chicago_consulting/
│   └── remote_distributed/
│
├── gen_alpha_student/
│   ├── us_public_high_school/
│   ├── us_private_school/
│   ├── us_middle_school/
│   └── international/
The Math
Without this insight:

Templates needed = Personas × Locations × Topics × Engines × Roles
                 = 2 × 10 × 10 × 4 × 4 = 3,200 templates
With this insight:

Templates needed = Personas × Topics × Engines × Roles
                 = 2 × 10 × 4 × 4 = 320 templates

Variable files = Personas × Locations × 4 files
               = 2 × 10 × 4 = 80 YAML files
Variable files are trivial compared to templates. Each is just a list of names, places, and trigger scenarios.

When You DO Need Separate Templates
Only when the voice itself changes, not just the details:

Same Template	Different Template
Gen Z in NYC vs Gen Z in SF	Gen Z vs Gen Alpha
Gen Z finance vs Gen Z tech	US English vs UK English
High school vs middle school	Formal professional vs casual creative
Example: How One Template Serves Multiple Locations
Template (same for all Gen Z):

markdown
{{character.name}} had been a {{character.role}} for {{character.years}} years.

The {{trigger.event}} came through at {{trigger.time}}.
{{trigger.context}}.

Nothing about it should have made their {{body.primary}}.
Variables — NYC Finance:

yaml
characters:
  - name: Jordan
    role: associate
    years: 2
    
triggers:
  - event: email from the MD
    time: 6 AM
    context: Two days before bonus announcements
Variables — SF Tech:

yaml
characters:
  - name: Jordan
    role: product manager
    years: 2
    
triggers:
  - event: Slack from the CEO
    time: 11 PM
    context: Week before the board meeting
Same template + different variables = different books, same voice quality.

What You Actually Need to Author
For Gen Z Professional:

1 template set (4 roles × topics × engines)
10 variable file sets (one per location)
For Gen Alpha Student:

1 template set
4-5 variable file sets (school types/regions)
Exception: Language/Culture Variants
Your file shows Chinese personas (Beijing, Shanghai, Taiwan). Those DO need separate templates because:

Different language
Different cultural references
Different relationship to mental health discussion
Different formality expectations
So:

gen_z_professional/en-US/ — one template set
gen_z_professional/zh-CN/ — separate template set (if serving Chinese market)
Bottom line: Templates define voice. Variables define world. Keep them separate and you scale efficiently.

Want me to create the variable file sets for all your Gen Z locations?

Jan 25

Assembled comprehensive template collection for healthcare nurses







Done. The Healthcare RN template set includes:

The Core Tension That Makes This Persona Work:

They know the physiology — can explain fight-or-flight to patients, draw the HPA axis diagram
That knowledge doesn't help when their own body won't stand down
Medical expertise becomes frustration, not relief
Variable Files:

Characters (6 profiles across units: med-surg, ICU, ED, L&D, cardiac, oncology)
Locations (nurses station, break room, supply room, parking garage, med room)
Triggers (call lights, overhead pages, monitor alarms, Vocera, rapid response)
Body sensations (with "related_action" — already moving before thinking)
Work context (patient loads, shift references, peer references)
Key Voice Elements:

Element	Healthcare RN
Knowledge of anxiety	High (clinical) — but it doesn't help
Unique insight	"Trained by years of real emergencies" — vigilance as competence
Coping attempts	Teaches breathing to patients, apps interrupted in break room
Resolution	"Functional" — realistic for someone in year 8 of floor nursing
Tension	Knows the science, can't apply it to self
The twist that lands for nurses:

"The alarm wasn't the problem. Her relationship to the alarm was the problem."

And:

"It wasn't broken. It was good at its job. Maybe too good. Maybe unable to clock out when she did."

That reframes hypervigilance as professional competence rather than personal failure — which is the specific shame-release nurses need.

Same templates serve ICU, ED, L&D, oncology, outpatient — just different variable files for setting-specific triggers and terminology.

      - "basement apartment in Ballard"

Jan 25

Analyzed file patterns and identified potential templating inconsistencies





I see a pattern emerging. Let me give you quick feedback:

What I'm Seeing
Topic-Variant Files (sv_techie__[topic], smb_owners__[topic])
These are all identical - same 3 opinion authors (Alex Rivera, Jordan Lee, Maya Chen) across every topic. Only the topic: field changes.

Potential Issue: These Gen Z opinion authors are being used for:

sv_techie (makes sense - could be Gen Z in tech)
smb_owners (⚠️ mismatch - SMB owners are typically older, not Gen Z)
Base Files
File	Author Count	Author Types	Assessment
sv_techie_authors.yaml	12	Professional (clinical, medical, wellness, leadership, etc.)	✅ Good variety
smb_owners_authors.yaml	12	Professional (same structure)	✅ Good variety
The Architecture Issue
You have two author types:

Professional authors (base files) - 2nd person POV, expert voices
Opinion authors (topic files) - 1st person POV, lived experience, Gen Z voice
The opinion authors are Gen Z-specific but being applied to all personas. For SMB owners, you might want Millennial/Gen X opinion authors with different pov_spine content about business ownership stress, not "7 years since high school."

Upload more! I want to see:

Location-specific files (like the NYC/Vancouver ones)
Any other persona base files
Sydney/Toronto Gen Alpha if you have them

what do you think? Dev: Location variables integrated into stories
What was done
1. Files copied (47 files):
* 35 base location files → SOURCE_OF_TRUTH/data/variables/locations/
* 12 generation-specific files → SOURCE_OF_TRUTH/data/variables/generation_locations/
1. Code created:
* location_variable_loader.py - Loads and merges location variables
* template_system.py - Updated to automatically load location variables
* generate_location_stories.py - Generates location-specific story variations
1. Integration tested:
* NYC Gen Z: 14 categories loaded
* NYC Gen Alpha: 13 categories loaded
* LA Gen Z: 11 categories loaded
* LA Gen Alpha: 13 categories loaded
How to use in stories
In templates
Add location variable placeholders:
{{character.name}} was {{character.age}}.
{{location.anchor}} in {{location.neighborhood}}, they felt it before they could name it.
The {{transit.transit_line}} was {{transit.transit_complaint}}.
They'd spent {{housing.monthly_rent}} on rent this month, and still lived in {{housing.dwelling_type}}.
{{culture.cheap_food}} for dinner again, because {{culture.expensive_food}} wasn't in the budget.
Automatic loading
When you use VariableHydrator.resolve_variables() with a location persona (e.g., "nyc_gen_z"), it automatically:
1. Parses persona to extract location and generation
1. Loads persona base variables
1. Loads base location variables
1. Loads generation-specific location variables
1. Merges all variables
Example output
NYC Gen Z:
* "room in a Bushwick apartment"
* "$1,850" rent
* "L train delays, packed cars, signal problems again"
* "dollar slice at 2am"
LA Gen Z:
* "studio in Silver Lake"
* "$2,200" rent
* "Red Line Metro"
* "taco truck or Thai Town $8 lunch"
Documentation created
1. LOCATION_VARIABLES_STORY_INTEGRATION.md - Complete integration guide
1. LOCATION_VARIABLES_INTEGRATION_COMPLETE.md - Implementation summary
1. HOW_TO_USE_LOCATION_VARIABLES_IN_STORIES.md - Usage guide
1. GAB_DEL_INTEGRATION_SUMMARY.md - Quick reference
Location variables are integrated and ready to use. Stories can now include location-specific details automatically when using location personas.

runtime_personas:
- id: sv_techie
  display_name: SV Techie
  voice_profile: direct, pragmatic, witty, kind
  default_region: us
  default_city: sf
  default_language: en-US
  tone_bias:
    business: 0.35
    scientific: 0.25
    funny: 0.2
    personal: 0.1
    mythic: 0.05
    spiritual: 0.05
  slang_ok: true
  localization_level: moderate
  personalization_rules:
  - rule: if book_topic == 'burnout'
    add_cues:
    - startup culture
    - VC pressure
    - demo day stress
    - crunch time
  - rule: if arc_stage == 'threshold' and story_type == 'personal'
    tone_adjustment: more vulnerable, less confident
  - rule: if story_type == 'funny'
    add_cues:
    - imposter syndrome
    - debugging hell
    - meeting fatigue
